					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Digital currency not reliant on a central authority</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Virtual_currency" title="Virtual currency">Virtual currency</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bitcoin.svg/183px-Bitcoin.svg.png" decoding="async" width="183" height="183" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bitcoin.svg/275px-Bitcoin.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bitcoin.svg/366px-Bitcoin.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></a><figcaption>A logo for <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin" title="Bitcoin">Bitcoin</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralized</a> cryptocurrency</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg/330px-Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="228" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg/500px-Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg/960px-Bitcoin-Genesis-block.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1342" data-file-height="927" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(blockchain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genesis (blockchain)">genesis block</a> of Bitcoin's <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">blockchain</a>, with a note containing <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> newspaper headline. This note has been interpreted as a comment on the instability caused by <a href="/wiki/Fractional-reserve_banking" title="Fractional-reserve banking">fractional-reserve banking</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">&#58;&#8202;18&#8202;</span></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>A <b>cryptocurrency</b> (colloquially <b>crypto</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Digital_currency" title="Digital currency">digital currency</a> designed to work through a <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">computer network</a> that is not reliant on any central authority, such as a <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">bank</a>, to uphold or maintain it.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Individual coin ownership records are stored in a digital <a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">ledger</a> or <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">blockchain</a>, which is a computerized <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a> that uses a consensus mechanism to secure <a href="/wiki/E-commerce" title="E-commerce">transaction</a> records, control the creation of additional coins, and verify the transfer of coin ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-crypto_currency_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crypto_currency-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reuterspricing_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuterspricing-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The two most common consensus mechanisms are <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_work" title="Proof of work">proof of work</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_stake" title="Proof of stake">proof of stake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite the name, which has come to describe many of the <a href="/wiki/Fungibility" title="Fungibility">fungible</a> blockchain tokens that have been created, cryptocurrencies are not considered to be <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currencies</a> in the traditional sense, and varying legal treatments have been applied to them in various jurisdicitons, including classification as <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a>, and currencies. Cryptocurrencies are generally viewed as a distinct asset class in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first cryptocurrency was <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin" title="Bitcoin">bitcoin</a>, which was first released as open-source software in 2009. As of June 2023, there were more than 25,000 <a href="/wiki/List_of_cryptocurrencies" title="List of cryptocurrencies">other cryptocurrencies</a> in the marketplace, of which more than 40 had a <a href="#Price_trends">market capitalization</a> exceeding $1&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_bitcoin" title="History of bitcoin">History of bitcoin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_bubble#History" title="Cryptocurrency bubble">Cryptocurrency bubble §&#160;History</a></div>
<p>In 1983, American <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptographer</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Chaum" title="David Chaum">David Chaum</a> conceived of a type of cryptographic <a href="/wiki/Electronic_money" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic money">electronic money</a> called <a href="/wiki/Ecash" title="Ecash">ecash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later, in 1995, he implemented it through <a href="/wiki/Digicash" class="mw-redirect" title="Digicash">Digicash</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an early form of cryptographic electronic payments. Digicash required user software in order to withdraw notes from a bank and designate specific encrypted keys before they could be sent to a recipient. This allowed the digital currency to be untraceable by a third party.
</p><p>In 1996, the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> published a paper entitled <i>How to Make a Mint: The Cryptography of Anonymous Electronic Cash</i>, describing a cryptocurrency system. The paper was first published in an <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a> mailing list (October 1996) and later (April 1997) in <i>The American Law Review</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, <a href="/wiki/Wei_Dai" title="Wei Dai">Wei Dai</a> described "b-money," an anonymous, distributed electronic cash system.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shortly thereafter, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Szabo" title="Nick Szabo">Nick Szabo</a> described <a href="/wiki/Bit_gold" class="mw-redirect" title="Bit gold">bit gold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies that would follow it, bit gold (not to be confused with the later gold-based exchange BitGold) was described as an electronic currency system that required users to complete a <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_work" title="Proof of work">proof of work</a> function with solutions being cryptographically put together and published.
</p><p>In January 2009, bitcoin was created by <a href="/wiki/Pseudonym" title="Pseudonym">pseudonymous</a> developer <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a>. It used <a href="/wiki/SHA-256" class="mw-redirect" title="SHA-256">SHA-256</a>, a cryptographic hash function, in its <a href="/wiki/Proof-of-work" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof-of-work">proof-of-work</a> scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In April 2011, <a href="/wiki/Namecoin" title="Namecoin">Namecoin</a> was created as an attempt at forming a decentralized <a href="/wiki/Domain_name_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Domain name system">DNS</a>. In October 2011, <a href="/wiki/Litecoin" title="Litecoin">Litecoin</a> was released, which used <a href="/wiki/Scrypt" title="Scrypt">scrypt</a> as its hash function instead of SHA-256. <a href="/wiki/Peercoin" title="Peercoin">Peercoin</a>, created in August 2012, used a hybrid of proof-of-work and <a href="/wiki/Proof-of-stake" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof-of-stake">proof-of-stake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steadman2013_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steadman2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Cryptocurrency has undergone several periods of growth and retraction, including several <a href="/wiki/Economic_bubble" title="Economic bubble">bubbles</a> and market crashes, such as in 2011, 2013–2014/15, 2017–2018, and 2021–2023.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 August 2014, the UK announced its <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> had commissioned a study of cryptocurrencies and what role, if any, they could play in the UK economy. The study was also to report on whether regulation should be considered.<sup id="cite_ref-Crypto_currencyUK_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crypto_currencyUK-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its final report was published in 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and it issued a consultation on cryptoassets and <a href="/wiki/Stablecoin" title="Stablecoin">stablecoins</a> in January 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2021, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> became the first country to accept bitcoin as <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_El_Salvador" title="Legislative Assembly of El Salvador">Legislative Assembly</a> had voted 62–22 to pass a bill submitted by President <a href="/wiki/Nayib_Bukele" title="Nayib Bukele">Nayib Bukele</a> classifying the cryptocurrency as such.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2021, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> followed with Resolution 215 to recognize and regulate cryptocurrencies such as bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2021, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_China" title="Government of China">government of China</a>, the single largest market for cryptocurrency, declared all cryptocurrency transactions illegal. This completed a crackdown on cryptocurrency that had previously banned the operation of intermediaries and miners within China.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 September 2022, the world's second largest cryptocurrency at that time, <a href="/wiki/Ethereum" title="Ethereum">Ethereum</a>, transitioned its <a href="/wiki/Consensus_(computer_science)" title="Consensus (computer science)">consensus mechanism</a> from <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_work" title="Proof of work">proof-of-work</a> (PoW) to <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_stake" title="Proof of stake">proof-of-stake</a> (PoS) in an upgrade process known as "the Merge".  According to the Ethereum Founder, the upgrade would cut both Ethereum's energy use and carbon-dioxide emissions by 99.9%.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 11 November 2022, <a href="/wiki/FTX_Trading_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="FTX Trading Ltd.">FTX Trading Ltd.</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_exchange" title="Cryptocurrency exchange">cryptocurrency exchange</a>, which also operated a crypto <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a>, and had been valued at $18 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-valued_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valued-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_proceedings" class="mw-redirect" title="Bankruptcy proceedings">filed</a> for <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_of_FTX" title="Bankruptcy of FTX">bankruptcy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-filed_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-filed-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The financial impact of the collapse extended beyond the immediate FTX customer base, as reported,<sup id="cite_ref-far_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-far-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while, at a <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> conference, financial industry executives said that "regulators must step in to protect crypto investors."<sup id="cite_ref-protect_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-protect-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Technology analyst Avivah Litan commented on the cryptocurrency ecosystem that "everything...needs to improve dramatically in terms of user experience, controls, safety, customer service."<sup id="cite_ref-ton_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ton-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Formal_definition">Formal definition</h2></div>
<p>According to Jan Lansky, a cryptocurrency is a system that meets six conditions:<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The system does not require a central authority; its state is maintained through distributed consensus.</li>
<li>The system keeps an overview of cryptocurrency units and their ownership.</li>
<li>The system defines whether new cryptocurrency units can be created. If new cryptocurrency units can be created, the system defines the circumstances of their origin and how to determine the ownership of these new units.</li>
<li>Ownership of cryptocurrency units can be proved exclusively cryptographically.</li>
<li>The system allows transactions to be performed in which ownership of the cryptographic units is changed. A transaction statement can only be issued by an entity proving the current ownership of these units.</li>
<li>If two different instructions for changing the ownership of the same cryptographic units are simultaneously entered, the system performs at most one of them.</li></ol>
<p>In March 2018, the word <i>cryptocurrency</i> was added to the <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Merriam-Webster Dictionary">Merriam-Webster Dictionary</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Altcoins">Altcoins</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cryptocurrencies" title="List of cryptocurrencies">List of cryptocurrencies</a></div>
<p>After the early innovation of bitcoin in 2008 and the early <a href="/wiki/Network_effect" title="Network effect">network effect</a> gained by bitcoin, tokens, cryptocurrencies, and other digital assets that were not bitcoin became collectively known during the 2010s as alternative cryptocurrencies,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or "altcoins".<sup id="cite_ref-aumatell2021_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aumatell2021-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Sometimes the term "alt coins" was used,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or disparagingly, "shitcoins".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Paul Vigna of <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> described altcoins in 2020 as "alternative versions of Bitcoin"<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> given its role as the model protocol for cryptocurrency designers. A <a href="/wiki/Polytechnic_University_of_Catalonia" title="Polytechnic University of Catalonia">Polytechnic University of Catalonia</a> thesis in 2021 used a broader description, including not only alternative versions of bitcoin but every cryptocurrency other than bitcoin. As of early 2020, there were more than 5,000 cryptocurrencies.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Eth-diamond-rainbow.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Eth-diamond-rainbow.png/220px-Eth-diamond-rainbow.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="367" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Eth-diamond-rainbow.png/330px-Eth-diamond-rainbow.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Eth-diamond-rainbow.png/440px-Eth-diamond-rainbow.png 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="3201" /></a><figcaption>The logo of Ethereum, the second largest cryptocurrency</figcaption></figure><p> Altcoins often have underlying differences when compared to bitcoin. For example, <a href="/wiki/Litecoin" title="Litecoin">Litecoin</a> aims to process a block every 2.5 minutes, rather than bitcoin's 10 minutes which allows Litecoin to confirm transactions faster than bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-Steadman2013_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steadman2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another example is <a href="/wiki/Ethereum" title="Ethereum">Ethereum</a>, which has <a href="/wiki/Smart_contract" title="Smart contract">smart contract</a> functionality that allows decentralized applications to be run on its blockchain.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ethereum was the most used blockchain in 2020, according to Bloomberg News.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2016, it had the largest "following" of any altcoin, according to the <i>New York Times</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Significant market price rallies across multiple altcoin markets are often referred to as an "altseason".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Stablecoins">Stablecoins</h4></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stablecoin" title="Stablecoin">Stablecoins</a> are cryptocurrencies designed to maintain a stable level of <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power" title="Purchasing power">purchasing power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notably, these designs are not foolproof, as a number of stablecoins have crashed or lost their <a href="/wiki/Fixed_exchange-rate_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Fixed exchange-rate system">peg</a>. For example, on 11 May 2022, <a href="/wiki/Terra_(blockchain)" title="Terra (blockchain)">Terra</a>'s stablecoin UST fell from $1 to 26 cents.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The subsequent failure of <a href="/wiki/Terraform_Labs" class="mw-redirect" title="Terraform Labs">Terraform Labs</a> resulted in the loss of nearly $40B invested in the Terra and Luna coins.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In September 2022, South Korean prosecutors requested the issuance of an <a href="/wiki/Interpol_red_notice" class="mw-redirect" title="Interpol red notice">Interpol Red Notice</a> against the company's founder, <a href="/wiki/Do_Kwon" title="Do Kwon">Do Kwon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Hong Kong, the expected regulatory framework for stablecoins in 2023/24 is being shaped and includes a few considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Memecoins">Memecoins</h4></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Meme_coin" title="Meme coin">Memecoins</a> are a category of cryptocurrencies that originated from <a href="/wiki/Internet_meme" title="Internet meme">Internet memes</a> or jokes. The most notable example is <a href="/wiki/Dogecoin" title="Dogecoin">Dogecoin</a>, a memecoin featuring the <a href="/wiki/Shiba_Inu" title="Shiba Inu">Shiba Inu</a> dog from the <a href="/wiki/Doge_(meme)" title="Doge (meme)">Doge</a> meme.<sup id="cite_ref-Yaffe-Bellany_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaffe-Bellany-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Memecoins are known for extreme volatility; for example, the record-high value for a Dogecoin was 73 cents, but that had plunged to 13 cents by mid-2024.<sup id="cite_ref-Yaffe-Bellany_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaffe-Bellany-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scams are prolific among memecoins.<sup id="cite_ref-Yaffe-Bellany_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaffe-Bellany-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Physical_crypto">Physical crypto</h2></div>
<p>Physical cryptocurrency coins have been made as promotional items and some have become collectibles.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of these have a private key embedded in them to access crypto worth a few dollars. There have also been attempts to issue bitcoin "bank notes".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term "physical bitcoin" is used in the finance industry when investment funds that hold crypto purchased from crypto exchanges put their crypto holdings in a specialised bank called a "custodian".<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These physical representations of cryptocurrency do not hold any value by themselves; these are only utilized for collectable purposes.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Architecture">Architecture</h2></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1251242444">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CFORK" class="mw-redirect" <p>Cryptocurrency is produced by an entire cryptocurrency system collectively, at a rate that is defined when the system is created and that is publicly stated. In centralized banking and economic systems such as the US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve System</a>, corporate boards or governments control the supply of currency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the case of cryptocurrency, companies or governments cannot produce new units and have not so far provided backing for other firms, banks, or corporate entities that hold asset value measured in it. The underlying technical system upon which cryptocurrencies are based was created by <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-te20151031_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-te20151031-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Within a <a href="/wiki/Proof-of-work" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof-of-work">proof-of-work</a> system such as bitcoin, the safety, integrity, and balance of <a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">ledgers</a> are maintained by a community of mutually distrustful parties referred to as <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_miners" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitcoin miners">miners</a>. Miners use their computers to help validate and timestamp transactions, adding them to the ledger in accordance with a particular timestamping scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Proof-of-stake" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof-of-stake">proof-of-stake</a> blockchain, transactions are validated by holders of the associated cryptocurrency, sometimes grouped together in stake pools.
</p><p>Most cryptocurrencies are designed to gradually decrease the production of that currency, placing a cap on the total amount of that currency that will ever be in circulation.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Compared with ordinary currencies held by financial institutions or kept as <a href="/wiki/Cash" title="Cash">cash</a> on hand, cryptocurrencies can be more difficult for <a href="/wiki/Search_and_seizure" title="Search and seizure">seizure</a> by law enforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-crypto_currency_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crypto_currency-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Blockchain">Blockchain</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">Blockchain</a></div>
<p>The validity of each cryptocurrency's coins is provided by a <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">blockchain</a>. A blockchain is a continuously growing list of <a href="/wiki/Record_(computer_science)" title="Record (computer science)">records</a>, called <i>blocks</i>, which are linked and secured using cryptography.<sup id="cite_ref-te20151031_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-te20151031-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cryptocurrencytech_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cryptocurrencytech-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each block typically contains a <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_hash_function" title="Cryptographic hash function">hash</a> pointer as a link to a previous block,<sup id="cite_ref-cryptocurrencytech_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cryptocurrencytech-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Trusted_timestamping" title="Trusted timestamping">timestamp</a>, and transaction data.<sup id="cite_ref-IPblockchain_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPblockchain-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By design, blockchains are inherently resistant to modification of the data. A blockchain is "an open, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_ledger" title="Distributed ledger">distributed ledger</a> that can record transactions between two parties efficiently and in a verifiable and permanent way".<sup id="cite_ref-hbr201701_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbr201701-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For use as a distributed ledger, a blockchain is typically managed by a <a href="/wiki/Peer-to-peer" title="Peer-to-peer">peer-to-peer</a> network collectively adhering to a protocol for validating new blocks. Once recorded, the data in any given block cannot be altered retroactively without the alteration of all subsequent blocks, which requires collusion of the network majority.
</p><p>Blockchains are <a href="/wiki/Secure_by_design" title="Secure by design">secure by design</a> and are an example of a distributed computing system with high <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_fault_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine fault tolerance">Byzantine fault tolerance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Decentralized" class="mw-redirect" title="Decentralized">Decentralized</a> consensus has therefore been achieved with a blockchain.<sup id="cite_ref-decapp_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decapp-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nodes">Nodes</h3></div>
<p>A <i>node</i> is a computer that connects to a cryptocurrency network. The node supports the cryptocurrency's network through either relaying transactions, validation, or hosting a copy of the blockchain. In terms of relaying transactions, each network computer (node) has a copy of the blockchain of the cryptocurrency it supports. When a transaction is made, the node creating the transaction broadcasts details of the transaction using encryption to other nodes throughout the node network so that the transaction (and every other transaction) is known.
</p><p>Node owners are either volunteers, those hosted by the organization or body responsible for developing the cryptocurrency blockchain network technology, or those who are enticed to host a node to receive rewards from hosting the node network.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Timestamping">Timestamping</h4></div>
<p>Cryptocurrencies use various timestamping schemes to "prove" the validity of transactions added to the blockchain ledger without the need for a trusted third party.
</p><p>The first timestamping scheme invented was the <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_work" title="Proof of work">proof-of-work</a> scheme. The most widely used proof-of-work schemes are based on SHA-256 and <a href="/wiki/Scrypt" title="Scrypt">scrypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steadman2013_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steadman2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some other hashing algorithms that are used for proof-of-work include <a href="/wiki/CryptoNote" title="CryptoNote">CryptoNote</a>, <a href="/wiki/BLAKE_(hash_function)" title="BLAKE (hash function)">Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/SHA-3" title="SHA-3">SHA-3</a>, and <a href="/wiki/X11_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="X11 algorithm">X11</a>.
</p><p>Another method is called the <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_stake" title="Proof of stake">proof-of-stake</a> scheme. Proof-of-stake is a method of securing a cryptocurrency network and achieving distributed consensus through requesting users to show ownership of a certain amount of currency. It is different from proof-of-work systems that run difficult hashing algorithms to validate electronic transactions. The scheme is largely dependent on the coin, and there is currently no standard form of it. Some cryptocurrencies use a combined proof-of-work and proof-of-stake scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-Steadman2013_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steadman2013-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mining">Mining</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg/250px-%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg/330px-%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg/500px-%D0%9E%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5_HashCoins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption>Hashcoin <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_network#Mining" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitcoin network">mine</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>On a blockchain, <i>mining</i> is the validation of transactions. For this effort, successful miners obtain new cryptocurrency as a reward. The reward decreases <a href="#Transaction_fees">transaction fees</a> by creating a complementary incentive to contribute to the processing power of the network. The rate of generating hashes, which validate any transaction, has been increased by the use of specialized hardware such as <a href="/wiki/Field-programmable_gate_array" title="Field-programmable gate array">FPGAs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Application-specific_integrated_circuit" title="Application-specific integrated circuit">ASICs</a> running complex hashing algorithms like SHA-256 and <a href="/wiki/Scrypt" title="Scrypt">scrypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-computer201709_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-computer201709-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This arms race for cheaper-yet-efficient machines has existed since bitcoin was introduced in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-computer201709_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-computer201709-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mining is measured by <a href="/wiki/Hashrate" title="Hashrate">hash rate</a>, typically in TH/s.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2023 <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a> working paper found that crypto mining could generate 450 million tons of CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> emissions by 2027, accounting for 0.7 percent of global emissions, or 1.2 percent of the world total<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With more people entering the world of virtual currency, generating hashes for validation has become more complex over time, forcing miners to invest increasingly large sums of money to improve computing performance. Consequently, the reward for finding a hash has diminished and often does not justify the investment in equipment and cooling facilities (to mitigate the heat the equipment produces) and the electricity required to run them.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Popular regions for mining include those with inexpensive electricity, a cold climate, and jurisdictions with clear and conducive regulations. By July 2019, bitcoin's electricity consumption was estimated to be approximately 7 gigawatts, around 0.2% of the global total, or equivalent to the energy consumed nationally by Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Mining_pool" title="Mining pool">miners pool resources</a>, sharing their <a href="/wiki/Computer_performance" title="Computer performance">processing power</a> over a network to split the reward equally, according to the amount of work they contributed to the probability of finding a <a href="/wiki/Blockchain_(database)" class="mw-redirect" title="Blockchain (database)">block</a>. A "share" is awarded to members of the mining pool who present a valid partial proof-of-work.
</p><p>As of February&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cryptocurrency&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the Chinese government has halted trading of virtual currency, banned initial coin offerings, and shut down mining. Many Chinese miners have since relocated to Canada<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One company is operating data centers for mining operations at Canadian oil and gas field sites due to low gas prices.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In June 2018, <a href="/wiki/Hydro-Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Hydro-Québec">Hydro Quebec</a> proposed to the provincial government to allocate 500 megawatts of power to crypto companies for mining.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to a February 2018 report from <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i>, Iceland has become a haven for cryptocurrency miners in part because of its cheap electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2018, the city of <a href="/wiki/Plattsburgh_(city),_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Plattsburgh (city), New York">Plattsburgh, New York</a> put an 18-month <a href="/wiki/Moratorium_(law)" title="Moratorium (law)">moratorium</a> on all cryptocurrency mining in an effort to preserve natural resources and the "character and direction" of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2021, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> became the second-biggest crypto-currency mining country, producing 18.1% of the global <a href="/wiki/Exahash" class="mw-redirect" title="Exahash">exahash</a> rate. The country built a compound containing 50,000 computers near <a href="/wiki/Ekibastuz" class="mw-redirect" title="Ekibastuz">Ekibastuz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="GPU_price_rise">GPU price rise</h4></div>
<p>An increase in cryptocurrency mining increased the demand for <a href="/wiki/Graphics_cards" class="mw-redirect" title="Graphics cards">graphics cards</a> (GPU) in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The computing power of GPUs makes them well-suited to generating hashes. Popular favorites of cryptocurrency miners, such as Nvidia's <a href="/wiki/GTX_1060" class="mw-redirect" title="GTX 1060">GTX 1060</a> and <a href="/wiki/GTX_1070" class="mw-redirect" title="GTX 1070">GTX 1070</a> graphics cards, as well as AMD's RX 570 and RX 580 GPUs, doubled or tripled in price&#160;&#8211;&#32;or were out of stock.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A GTX 1070 Ti, which was released at a price of $450, sold for as much as $1,100. Another popular card, the GTX 1060 (6 GB model), was released at an <a href="/wiki/MSRP" class="mw-redirect" title="MSRP">MSRP</a> of $250 and sold for almost $500. RX 570 and RX 580 cards from <a href="/wiki/AMD" title="AMD">AMD</a> were out of stock for almost a year. Miners regularly buy up the entire stock of new GPUs as soon as they are available.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a> has asked retailers to do what they can when it comes to selling GPUs to gamers instead of miners. Boris Böhles, PR manager for Nvidia in the German region, said: "Gamers come first for Nvidia."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Mining_accelerator_chips">Mining accelerator chips</h4></div>
<p>Numerous companies developed dedicated crypto-mining accelerator chips, capable of price-performance far higher than that of CPU or <a href="/wiki/GPU_mining" title="GPU mining">GPU mining</a>. At one point, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> marketed its own brand of crypto accelerator chip, named <a href="/wiki/Blockscale" title="Blockscale">Blockscale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Wallets">Wallets</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png/250px-A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="64" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png/330px-A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png/500px-A_paper_printable_Bitcoin_wallet_consisting_of_one_bitcoin_address_for_receiving_and_the_corresponding_private_key_for_spending.png 2x" data-file-width="2643" data-file-height="770" /></a><figcaption>An example paper printable Bitcoin wallet consisting of one Bitcoin address for receiving and the corresponding private key for spending</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_wallet" title="Cryptocurrency wallet">Cryptocurrency wallet</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_wallet" title="Cryptocurrency wallet">cryptocurrency wallet</a> is a means of storing the <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">public and private "keys"</a> (address) or seed, which can be used to receive or spend the cryptocurrency.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the private key, it is possible to write in the public ledger, effectively spending the associated cryptocurrency. With the public key, it is possible for others to send currency to the wallet.
</p><p>There exist multiple methods of storing keys or seed in a wallet. These methods range from using paper wallets (which are public, private, or seed keys written on paper), to using hardware wallets (which are hardware to store your wallet information), to a digital wallet (which is a computer with software hosting your wallet information), to hosting your wallet using an exchange where cryptocurrency is traded, or by storing your wallet information on a digital medium such as plaintext.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Anonymity">Anonymity</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_and_blockchain" title="Privacy and blockchain">Privacy and blockchain</a></div>
<p>Bitcoin is <a href="/wiki/Pseudonymous" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudonymous">pseudonymous</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Anonymity" title="Anonymity">anonymous</a>; the cryptocurrency in a wallet is not tied to a person but rather to one or more specific keys (or "addresses").<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thereby, bitcoin owners are not immediately identifiable, but all transactions are publicly available in the blockchain.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Still, <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_exchanges" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptocurrency exchanges">cryptocurrency exchanges</a> are often required by law to collect the personal information of their users.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some cryptocurrencies, such as <a href="/wiki/Monero" title="Monero">Monero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zerocoin" class="mw-redirect" title="Zerocoin">Zerocoin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zerocash" class="mw-redirect" title="Zerocash">Zerocash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/CryptoNote" title="CryptoNote">CryptoNote</a>, implement additional measures to increase privacy, such as by using <a href="/wiki/Zero-knowledge_proofs" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-knowledge proofs">zero-knowledge proofs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A recent 2020 study presented different attacks on privacy in cryptocurrencies. The attacks demonstrated how the anonymity techniques are not sufficient safeguards. In order to improve privacy, researchers suggested several different ideas, including new cryptographic schemes and mechanisms for hiding the <a href="/wiki/IP_address" title="IP address">IP address</a> of the source.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Economics">Economics</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cryptoeconomics" title="Cryptoeconomics">Cryptoeconomics</a></div>
<p>Cryptocurrencies are used primarily outside banking and governmental institutions and are exchanged over the Internet.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Block_rewards">Block rewards</h3></div>
<p>Proof-of-work cryptocurrencies, such as bitcoin, offer block rewards incentives for miners. There has been an implicit belief that whether miners are paid by block rewards or transaction fees does not affect the security of the blockchain, but a study suggests that this may not be the case under certain circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The rewards paid to miners increase the supply of the cryptocurrency. By making sure that verifying transactions is a costly business, the integrity of the network can be preserved as long as benevolent nodes control a majority of computing power. The verification algorithm requires a lot of processing power, and thus electricity, in order to make verification costly enough to accurately validate the public blockchain. Not only do miners have to factor in the costs associated with expensive equipment necessary to stand a chance of solving a hash problem, they must further consider the significant amount of electrical power in search of the solution. Generally, the block rewards outweigh electricity and equipment costs, but this may not always be the case.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The current value, not the long-term value, of the cryptocurrency supports the reward scheme to incentivize miners to engage in costly mining activities.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2018, bitcoin's design caused a 1.4% welfare loss compared to an efficient cash system, while a cash system with 2% money growth has a minor 0.003% welfare cost. The main source for this inefficiency is the large mining cost, which is estimated to be US$360 million per year. This translates into users being willing to accept a cash system with an inflation rate of 230% before being better off using bitcoin as a means of payment. However, the efficiency of the bitcoin system can be significantly improved by optimizing the rate of coin creation and minimizing transaction fees. Another potential improvement is to eliminate inefficient mining activities by changing the consensus protocol altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Transaction_fees">Transaction fees</h3></div>
<p>Transaction fees (sometimes also referred to as <i>miner fees</i> or <i>gas fees</i>) for cryptocurrency depend mainly on the <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply</a> of network capacity at the time, versus the <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">demand</a> from the currency holder for a faster transaction.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ability for the holder to be allowed to set the fee manually often depends on the wallet software used, and central <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_exchange" title="Cryptocurrency exchange">exchanges for cryptocurrency</a> (CEX) usually do not allow the customer to set a custom transaction fee for the transaction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Their wallet software, such as <a href="/wiki/Coinbase" title="Coinbase">Coinbase</a> Wallet, however, might support adjusting the fee.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Select cryptocurrency exchanges have offered to let the user choose between different presets of transaction fee values during the currency conversion. One of those exchanges, namely <b>LiteBit</b>, previously headquartered in the Netherlands, was forced to cease all operations on August 13th, 2023, "due to market changes and regulatory pressure".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The "recommended fee" suggested by the network will often depend on the time of day (due to depending on network load).
</p><p>For <a href="/wiki/Ethereum" title="Ethereum">Ethereum</a>, transaction fees differ by computational complexity, bandwidth use, and storage needs, while bitcoin transaction fees differ by transaction size and whether the transaction uses <a href="/wiki/SegWit" title="SegWit">SegWit</a>. In February 2023, the median transaction fee for Ether corresponded to $2.2845,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while for bitcoin it corresponded to $0.659.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some cryptocurrencies have no transaction fees, the most well-known example being <a href="/wiki/Nano_(cryptocurrency)" title="Nano (cryptocurrency)">Nano (XNO)</a>, and instead rely on <a href="/wiki/Client-side" class="mw-redirect" title="Client-side">client-side</a> proof-of-work as the transaction prioritization and anti-spam mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-scalabilityOfBitcoinAndNano_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scalabilityOfBitcoinAndNano-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-”comparativeAnalysis”_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-”comparativeAnalysis”-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blockchainCompared_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blockchainCompared-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Exchanges">Exchanges</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_exchange" title="Cryptocurrency exchange">Cryptocurrency exchange</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_exchange" title="Cryptocurrency exchange">Cryptocurrency exchanges</a> allow customers to trade cryptocurrencies<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> for other assets, such as conventional <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">fiat money</a>, or to trade between different digital currencies.
</p><p>Crypto marketplaces do not guarantee that an investor is completing a purchase or trade at the optimal price. As a result, as of 2020, it was possible to <a href="/wiki/Arbitrage" title="Arbitrage">arbitrage</a> to find the difference in price across several markets.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Atomic_swaps">Atomic swaps</h3></div>
<p>Atomic swaps are a mechanism where one cryptocurrency can be exchanged directly for another cryptocurrency without the need for a trusted third party, such as an exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="ATMs">ATMs</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg/170px-Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg/255px-Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg/340px-Bitcoin_Teller_SIBOS_Boston_2014_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_ATM" title="Bitcoin ATM">Bitcoin ATM</a> </figcaption></figure>
<p>Jordan Kelley, founder of <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_ATM" title="Bitcoin ATM">Robocoin</a>, launched the first <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_ATM" title="Bitcoin ATM">bitcoin ATM</a> in the United States on 20 February 2014. The kiosk installed in Austin, Texas, is similar to bank ATMs but has scanners to read government-issued identification such as a driver's license or a passport to confirm users' identities.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Initial_coin_offerings">Initial coin offerings</h3></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Initial_coin_offering" title="Initial coin offering">initial coin offering</a> (ICO) is a controversial means of raising funds for a new cryptocurrency venture. An ICO may be used by startups with the intention of avoiding regulation. However, securities regulators in many jurisdictions, including in the U.S. and Canada, have indicated that if a coin or token is an "investment contract" (e.g., under the Howey test, i.e., an investment of money with a reasonable expectation of profit based significantly on the entrepreneurial or managerial efforts of others), it is a security and is subject to securities regulation. In an ICO campaign, a percentage of the cryptocurrency (usually in the form of "tokens") is sold to early backers of the project in exchange for legal tender or other cryptocurrencies, often bitcoin or Ether.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/PricewaterhouseCoopers" class="mw-redirect" title="PricewaterhouseCoopers">PricewaterhouseCoopers</a>, four of the 10 biggest proposed initial coin offerings have used <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> as a base, where they are frequently registered as non-profit foundations. The Swiss regulatory agency <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Financial_Market_Supervisory_Authority" title="Swiss Financial Market Supervisory Authority">FINMA</a> stated that it would take a "balanced approach" to ICO projects and would allow "legitimate innovators to navigate the regulatory landscape and so launch their projects in a way consistent with national laws protecting investors and the integrity of the financial system." In response to numerous requests by industry representatives, a legislative ICO working group began to issue legal guidelines in 2018, which are intended to remove uncertainty from cryptocurrency offerings and to establish sustainable business practices.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Price_trends">Price trends</h3></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Market_capitalization" title="Market capitalization">market capitalization</a> of a cryptocurrency is calculated by multiplying the price by the number of coins in circulation. The total cryptocurrency market cap has historically been dominated by bitcoin accounting for at least 50% of the market cap value where altcoins have increased and decreased in market cap value in relation to bitcoin. Bitcoin's value is largely determined by speculation among other technological limiting factors known as blockchain rewards coded into the architecture technology of bitcoin itself. The cryptocurrency market cap follows a trend known as the "halving", which is when the block rewards received from bitcoin are halved due to technological mandated limited factors instilled into bitcoin which in turn limits the supply of bitcoin. As the date reaches near of a halving (twice thus far historically) the cryptocurrency market cap increases, followed by a downtrend.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By June 2021, cryptocurrency had begun to be offered by some wealth managers in the US for <a href="/wiki/401(k)" title="401(k)">401(k)s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Volatility">Volatility</h3></div>
<p>Cryptocurrency prices are much more volatile than established financial assets such as <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">stocks</a>. For example, over one week in May 2022, bitcoin lost 20% of its value and Ethereum lost 26%, while <a href="/wiki/Solana_(blockchain_platform)" title="Solana (blockchain platform)">Solana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardano_(blockchain_platform)" title="Cardano (blockchain platform)">Cardano</a> lost 41% and 35% respectively. The falls were attributed to warnings about inflation. By comparison, in the same week, the <a href="/wiki/Nasdaq-100" title="Nasdaq-100">Nasdaq</a> tech stock index fell 7.6 per cent and the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100</a> was 3.6 per cent down.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the longer term, of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cryptocurrencies" title="List of cryptocurrencies">10 leading cryptocurrencies</a> identified by the total value of coins in circulation in January 2018, only four (bitcoin, Ethereum, Cardano and <a href="/wiki/Ripple_(payment_protocol)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ripple (payment protocol)">Ripple</a> (XRP)) were still in that position in early 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The total value of all cryptocurrencies was  $2 trillion at the end of 2021, but had halved nine months later.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Wall Street Journal</i> has commented that the crypto sector has become "intertwined" with the rest of the capital markets and "sensitive to the same forces that drive tech stocks and other risk assets," such as inflation forecasts.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Databases">Databases</h3></div>
<p>There are also <i>centralized</i> databases, outside of blockchains, that store crypto market data. Compared to the blockchain, databases perform fast as there is no verification process. Four of the most popular cryptocurrency market databases are CoinMarketCap, CoinGecko, BraveNewCoin, and Cryptocompare.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Social_and_political_aspects">Social and political aspects</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Crypto-anarchism" class="mw-redirect" title="Crypto-anarchism">Crypto-anarchism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cypherpunk" title="Cypherpunk">Cypherpunk</a></div>
<p>According to Alan Feuer of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Libertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian">libertarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-capitalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarcho-capitalist">anarcho-capitalists</a> were attracted to the philosophical idea behind bitcoin. Early bitcoin supporter <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ver" title="Roger Ver">Roger Ver</a> said: "At first, almost everyone who got involved did so for philosophical reasons. We saw bitcoin as a great idea, as a way to separate money from the state."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Economist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> argues that cryptocurrencies like bitcoin are "something of a cult" based in "paranoid fantasies" of government power.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>David Golumbia says that the ideas influencing bitcoin advocates emerge from right-wing extremist movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Lobby" title="Liberty Lobby">Liberty Lobby</a> and the <a href="/wiki/John_Birch_Society" title="John Birch Society">John Birch Society</a> and their anti-Central Bank rhetoric, or, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul" title="Ron Paul">Ron Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party</a>-style libertarianism.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bannon" title="Steve Bannon">Steve Bannon</a>, who owns a "good stake" in bitcoin, sees cryptocurrency as a form of disruptive populism, taking control back from central authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bitcoin's founder, <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a>, supported the idea that cryptocurrencies go well with libertarianism. "It's very attractive to the libertarian viewpoint if we can explain it properly," Nakamoto said in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, the decentralization of money offered by bitcoin has its theoretical roots in the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_school_of_economics" title="Austrian school of economics">Austrian school of economics</a>, especially with <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Hayek" class="mw-redirect" title="Friedrich von Hayek">Friedrich von Hayek</a> in his book <i>Denationalisation of Money: The Argument Refined</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in which Hayek advocates a complete <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> in the production, distribution and management of money to end the monopoly of <a href="/wiki/Central_banks" class="mw-redirect" title="Central banks">central banks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Regulation">Regulation</h2></div>
<p>The rise in the popularity of cryptocurrencies and their adoption by financial institutions has led some governments to assess whether regulation is needed to protect users. The <a href="/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force" title="Financial Action Task Force">Financial Action Task Force</a> (FATF) has defined cryptocurrency-related services as "virtual asset service providers" (VASPs) and recommended that they be regulated with the same <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a> (<a href="/wiki/Money_laundering#Combating" title="Money laundering">AML</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Know_your_customer" title="Know your customer">know your customer</a> (KYC) requirements as financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2020, the Joint Working Group on interVASP Messaging Standards published "IVMS 101", a universal common language for communication of required originator and beneficiary information between VASPs. The FATF and financial regulators were informed as the data model was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2020, FATF updated its guidance to include the "Travel Rule" for cryptocurrencies, a measure which mandates that VASPs obtain, hold, and exchange information about the originators and beneficiaries of virtual asset transfers.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Subsequent standardized protocol specifications recommended using <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> for relaying data between VASPs and identity services. As of December 2020, the IVMS 101 data model has yet to be finalized and ratified by the three global standard setting bodies that created it.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The European Commission published a digital finance strategy in September 2020. This included a draft regulation on Markets in Crypto-Assets (MiCA), which aimed to provide a comprehensive regulatory framework for digital assets in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 10 June 2021, the <a href="/wiki/Basel_Committee_on_Banking_Supervision" title="Basel Committee on Banking Supervision">Basel Committee on Banking Supervision</a> proposed that banks that held cryptocurrency assets must set aside capital to cover all potential losses. For instance, if a bank were to hold bitcoin worth $2 billion, it would be required to set aside enough capital to cover the entire $2 billion. This is a more extreme standard than banks are usually held to when it comes to other assets. However, this is a proposal and not a regulation.
</p><p>The IMF is seeking a coordinated, consistent and comprehensive approach to supervising cryptocurrencies. <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Adrian" title="Tobias Adrian">Tobias Adrian</a>, the IMF's financial counsellor and head of its monetary and capital markets department said in a January 2022 interview that "Agreeing global regulations is never quick. But if we start now, we can achieve the goal of maintaining financial stability while also enjoying the benefits which the underlying technological innovations bring,"<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2024, 15 years after the advent of the first blockchain, bitcoin, the <a href="/wiki/US_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="US Congress">US Congress</a> advanced a bill to the full <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> to provide regulatory clarity for digital assets.
